  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_9c93dbd7-38df-4d82-a3b3-f45f4609f997'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10493787718887176777">At the beginning of <i>Pudd'nhead Wilson</i> a young slave woman, fearing for her infant's son's life, exchanges her light-skinned child with her master's.  From this rather simple premise Mark Twain fashioned one of his most entertaining, funny, yet biting novels.  On its surface, <i>Pudd'nhead Wilson</i> possesses all the elements of an engrossing nineteenth-century mystery:  reversed</span>
  <span id="freeText10493787718887176777" style="display:none">At the beginning of <i>Pudd'nhead Wilson</i> a young slave woman, fearing for her infant's son's life, exchanges her light-skinned child with her master's.  From this rather simple premise Mark Twain fashioned one of his most entertaining, funny, yet biting novels.  On its surface, <i>Pudd'nhead Wilson</i> possesses all the elements of an engrossing nineteenth-century mystery:  reversed identities, a horrible crime, an eccentric detective, a suspenseful courtroom drama, and a surprising, unusual solution.  Yet it is not a mystery novel.  Seething with the undercurrents of antebellum southern culture, the book is a savage indictment in which the real criminal is society, and racial prejudice and slavery are the crimes.  Written in 1894, <i>Pudd'nhead Wilson</i> glistens with characteristic Twain humor, with suspense, and with pointed irony:  a gem among the author's later works.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10493787718887176777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1655877539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1655877539" />
    <a title="Henry Avila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila"><img alt="Henry Avila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307597403p2/5431458.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1655877539?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henry Avila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henry Avila" href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila">Henry Avila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1655877539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18314109193110010595">During the antebellum south on the western shore of the broad, mighty , muddy Mississippi River 2,350 miles long and miles wide, in the golden era of the steamboats ( numbering an astounding 1,200, vessels ) feed by more than a dozen tributaries, they continuously went up and down those waters and entered other streams too. A small , tranquil village named Dawson's Landing stood, half a days travel by boat below St.Louis in the state of Missouri not an important place mind you, yet when an intri</span>
  <span id="freeText18314109193110010595" style="display:none">During the antebellum south on the western shore of the broad, mighty , muddy Mississippi River 2,350 miles long and miles wide, in the golden era of the steamboats ( numbering an astounding 1,200, vessels ) feed by more than a dozen tributaries, they continuously went up and down those waters and entered other streams too. A small , tranquil village named Dawson's Landing stood, half a days travel by boat below St.Louis in the state of Missouri not an important place mind you, yet when an intriguing stranger, Mr.David Wilson arrives from a distant part of the country, seeking to practice law here things pick up, he hears loud barking from an unseen dog on his first day, annoyed greatly the new lawyer tells some curious leading citizens that if he owned half the animal he Wilson would kill it... Shortly afterwards the influential people in town gather and discuss those quite remarkable silly words by the newcomer, after a short conversation come to the unanimous conclusion this Mr.Wilson is a pudd'nhead (translation, an idiot) the unfortunate man quickly receives that nickname Pudd'nhead, his high hopes for being a successful lawyer collapses , no one will hire this obvious moron...At about the same time two look alike children are born both boys, one to Percy Driscoll, Thomas Driscoll, from a prominent family in old Virginia his poor wife soon expires in the effort, another from a black slave the beautiful Roxana ( no surnames given them) her son, she names Valet de Chambers. The interesting thing is Roxana is white in appearance, only one -sixteenth black which makes Chambers one -thirty -second he had a white father however one drop of blood , will change your life. Roxy who takes care of the children, is afraid she and her baby will he sold and sent down the river to a fearful fate , by her owner Percy Driscoll and switches children Thomas becomes Chambers and Chambers , Thomas, nobody notices the difference, still one has a fabulous life of wealth and privilege the fake Tom, ( doesn't known he's black) is a cruel vindictive, coward the real Tom a kind, generous, forgiving , brave man suffers misery , humiliation and beatings frequently by the impostor yes, treated like a slave.... Mr. Wilson has plenty of time for a hobby between doing odd jobs in accounting and surveying to survive, virtually unknown to the public fingerprinting, a system that can identify anyone by the patterns on the tips of your fingers, he takes the prints of all on a glass in the village, the amused citizens think just another eccentricity of the addle- brained man, then a mystery happens, local robberies occur in the quiet, peaceful town . More fascinating the enigmatic Italian twins , Luigi and Angelo Capello nobles they say, somehow find this tiny community through a newspaper ad and rent a room, the entire Dawson's Landing is thrilled some excitement finally here, later duels, a murder and an old woman who keeps being seen and vanishing at the site of further unexplained, petty robberies ...A fine story that starts as a comedy and then unexpectedly turns serious, telling and showing the tragedy of slavery... Mark Twain wrote about this evil institution and reached the American conscious.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18314109193110010595" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2309578692" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2309578692" />
    <a title="mark monday" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday"><img alt="mark monday" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572672245p2/131922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2309578692?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mark monday" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mark monday" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday">mark monday</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486408859" href="/book/show/236952.Pudd_nhead_Wilson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=these-fragile-lives">these-fragile-lives</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2309578692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer944165158609196006"><u>Samuel Langhorne Clemens</u>: I shall write a classic novel, full of my customary barbed wit yet leavened with my compassion for humanity. I shall open the tale with a delightfully wry meta-introduction - before "meta" was even a thing! The wryness shall continue throughout what will be an exciting story of bold misdeeds, uncertain justice, and a compelling and surely very surprising trial. We shall end the tale with evil happily circumvented - but it will be an ending that is also dripping with iro</span>
  <span id="freeText944165158609196006" style="display:none"><u>Samuel Langhorne Clemens</u>: I shall write a classic novel, full of my customary barbed wit yet leavened with my compassion for humanity. I shall open the tale with a delightfully wry meta-introduction - before "meta" was even a thing! The wryness shall continue throughout what will be an exciting story of bold misdeeds, uncertain justice, and a compelling and surely very surprising trial. We shall end the tale with evil happily circumvented - but it will be an ending that is also dripping with irony and pointed critique. An important fact: as a classic progressive, I have always been morally opposed to slavery and adamantly in favor of emancipation; likewise I firmly believe in enhancing the rights of former slaves and their descendants. This revolutionary perspective will be present in my tale - but it shall be a trifle muted, to allow for brisk sales.<br /><br /><u>Pudd'nhead Wilson</u>: I am the moral center of this tale and I shall hold that title with much becoming humility. I shall charm the reader with my unusual observations, sly comments, humane nature, and my prescient knowledge and use of finger-printing - all of this despite the derision of my fellows. I am perhaps a stand-in for the estimable Mark Twain. More importantly, I am also what is known as an Underdog. Rally behind me!<br /><br /><u>The Italian Twins</u>: We came from a discarded story, where we were once conjoined. But this tale has set us free! Pudd'nhead Wilson may be the hero of the piece, but our joie de vivre, pluck, style, and the utter fun we bring to this tale of dark deeds shall surely make us a favorite among certain lady readers - and certain reviewers like mark monday!<br /><br /><u>Valet de Chambre AKA Tom Driscoll</u>: I am the villain of the piece - but I shall rally against such diminishing, unempathetic designations! I am only human, after all. I shall enrage the reader with my high-handed, bullying ways, my cunning and greedy nature, my cheeky aplomb, my devious misdeeds done in the dark of night. I am what is known as a changeling, a cuckoo's offspring, an interloper. I am an argument in favor of nurture over nature: it is the spoiling, too-generous nurture of my uncle and aunt that shall sour my nature and turn me into a braggart, gambler, and vindictive villain. Or is this truly the case? Even as a babe in arms, I am characterized by my monstrousness... surely this is not due to my blackness, if being 1/32 part black even constitutes "blackness"? Unfortunately, the author could have been rather more clear on where my innately bad nature sprung from. That lack of clarity certainly muddies the water a bit.<br /><br /><u>Roxie</u>: "I's sorry for you, honey; I's sorry, God knows I is, - but what <i>kin</i> I do, what <i>could</i> I do? Yo' pappy would sell him to somebody, some time, en den he'd go down de river, sho', en I couldn't, couldn't, <i>couldn't</i> stan' it... 'Tain't no sin - <i>white</i> folks has done it! It ain't no sin, glory to goodness it ain't no sin! <i>Dey's</i> done it - yes, en dey was de biggest quality in de whole bilin', too - <i>kings</i>!"<br /><br /><u>mark monday</u>: I thought this was an admirable tale in many ways, well-written and enjoyable, with a leisurely but exciting narrative. However - despite its good, progressive intentions - the cloudiness at the story's center, its confusion around "nature vs. nurture", made me increasingly uncomfortable. And reading Roxie's dialogue and monologues - despite being true to place and time - was completely excruciating, at least to these modern eyes.<br /><br /><br /><br />5 of 16 in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/946650.Sixteen_Short_Novels" title="Sixteen Short Novels by Wilfrid Sheed" rel="nofollow noopener">Sixteen Short Novels</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="944165158609196006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_26543419" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26543419" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kressel Housman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kressel Housman" href="/user/show/575806-kressel-housman">Kressel Housman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/575806-kressel-housman?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26543419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3295825611015333674"><i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2956.The_Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn' title='The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn by Mark Twain' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</a>
</i> has biting social commentary, but <i>Puddin'head Wilson</i> has all-out black humor. It's the story of Roxy, a light-skinned slave woman who successfully switches her even lighter-skinned son with her master's baby, and follows how each one grows up. I would have liked to see more inside the slaves' lives other than from the character of Roxy, but Mark Twain's point was mainly to criticize the spoiled slaveowners. In any case, the courtroom drama in which Puddin'h</span>
  <span id="freeText3295825611015333674" style="display:none"><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2956.The_Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" title="The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn by Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</a>
</i> has biting social commentary, but <i>Puddin'head Wilson</i> has all-out black humor. It's the story of Roxy, a light-skinned slave woman who successfully switches her even lighter-skinned son with her master's baby, and follows how each one grows up. I would have liked to see more inside the slaves' lives other than from the character of Roxy, but Mark Twain's point was mainly to criticize the spoiled slaveowners. In any case, the courtroom drama in which Puddin'head Wilson reveals the truth will have you riveted.<br /> <br />An interesting literary tidbit: Mark Twain is known to have disliked Jane Austen's work, saying something roughly along these lines, "I can't stand <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1265.Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Austen</a>. Every time I read <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1885.Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice by Jane Austen" rel="nofollow noopener">Pride and Prejudice</a>,</i> I want to dig her up and beat her with her own shin bone." But as the Jane Austen fans love to point out, "every time I read . . ." implies that he read her more than once, and Mark Twain's sense of humor was to be negative about everybody and everything. But I think his ultimate tribute to JA comes at the beginning of <i>Puddin'head</i>. Compare this: "There is no character, howsoever good and fine, but it can be destroyed by ridicule, howsoever poor and witless" to Darcy's "the wisest and the best of men - nay, the wisest and best of their actions - may be rendered ridiculous by a person whose first object in life is a joke." Perhaps it's just a universal observation by two satirists, but I think the wording is very close.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3295825611015333674" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2052765171" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2052765171?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="LaDonna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="LaDonna" href="/user/show/66577665-ladonna">LaDonna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66577665-ladonna?shelf=2017-reading-challenge">2017-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66577665-ladonna?shelf=african-american-diaspora">african-american-diaspora</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66577665-ladonna?shelf=a-must-read">a-must-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66577665-ladonna?shelf=goodreads">goodreads</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2052765171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9845524798978428792"><b>
  <i>WOW!!</i>
</b> Without divulging any spoilers, that was my reaction to the last sentence of Mark Twain's <i>Pudd'nhead Wilson</i>. Suffice it to say that the book took several twists and turns that I did not see coming, but each of them definitely kept the story moving.<br/><br/>There was no way I was going to pass on an opportunity to read a book with a premise such a this one: A white man, born free, but switched at 7 months of age to be raised as a slave. A black man, born into slavery, but switched at 7 months of</span>
  <span id="freeText9845524798978428792" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>WOW!!</i>
</b> Without divulging any spoilers, that was my reaction to the last sentence of Mark Twain's <i>Pudd'nhead Wilson</i>. Suffice it to say that the book took several twists and turns that I did not see coming, but each of them definitely kept the story moving.<br /><br />There was no way I was going to pass on an opportunity to read a book with a premise such a this one: A white man, born free, but switched at 7 months of age to be raised as a slave. A black man, born into slavery, but switched at 7 months of age to be raised as a slave owner. What a freakin' social experiment!! <b>
  <i> A devil born to a young couple is measurably recognizable by them as a devil before long, but a devil adopted by an old couple is an angel to them, and remains so, through thick and thin.</i>
</b> First published in 1894, Twain manages to truly challenge the idea of nature versus nurture, using both humor and suspnse. (Twain even manages to throw in some forensics science for good measure).<br /><br />In a the words of Langston Hughes, author of this particular edition's introduction, <blockquote>
  <b> <i> In Pudd'nhead Wilson</i> Mark Twain wrote what a later period might have been called in the finest sense of the term, a novel of social significance...Twain minces no words in describing the unfortunate effects of slavery upon the behavior of both Negroes and whites, even upon children. </b>
</blockquote><br />I am quite sure that most of us read <i>The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</i> in an English Lit. class, somewhere along the line. But, personally, I would have loved to have read this one. It has been well over 100 years since Mark Twain created this work; yet, it still speaks to criminality our society condones and the racial prejudices that still exist. Even Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar has an adage that speaks to the importance of embracing differences, <b>
  <i>It were not best that we should all think alike; it is difference of opinion that makes horse-races.</i>
</b><br /><br />If you have not guessed, this book is definitely on my must-read bookshelf. It will truly leave you wondering: Where do we go from here? And, how can we make things better for everyone?<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Courage is resistance to fear, mastery of fear--not the absence of fear</i>
</b>--<i>Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="9845524798978428792" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3135492345" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3135492345" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3135492345?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780195114157" href="/book/show/2834.The_Tragedy_of_Pudd_nhead_Wilson_Those_Extraordinary_Twins">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3135492345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2317589007128179030">3.5, rounded up. Not my favorite Twain, but quite worth the reading.<br/><br/>Pudd’nhead Wilson is a tragedy masquerading as a farce, or maybe a farce masquerading as a tragedy. As was always true with Twain, he writes comedy that is so cutting that it can barely mask the underlying seriousness of his subject. The subject is slavery, and the farce is necessary, for the tragedy is real.<br/><br/>In this novel, two babies are switched at birth, one a master the other a slave, and through that prism we are able to vie</span>
  <span id="freeText2317589007128179030" style="display:none">3.5, rounded up. Not my favorite Twain, but quite worth the reading.<br /><br />Pudd’nhead Wilson is a tragedy masquerading as a farce, or maybe a farce masquerading as a tragedy. As was always true with Twain, he writes comedy that is so cutting that it can barely mask the underlying seriousness of his subject. The subject is slavery, and the farce is necessary, for the tragedy is real.<br /><br />In this novel, two babies are switched at birth, one a master the other a slave, and through that prism we are able to view some important contrasts--nature vs. nurture, loyalty vs. betrayal, and a mother’s love vs. a father’s indifference. Twain is at home with this device, as he loves to turn tails on his characters: The Connecticut Yankee who finds himself in another century; the Prince and the Pauper, another set of switched children; even Huck, who finds himself transported from a world in which slavery is the norm to one in which a man can be set free. But this is his most ambitious switch-up, because this switch touches at the core of what makes a man who he is.<br /><br />Beyond the racial theme is the theme of loyalty and betrayal that is truly stark and brutal. There is one event in the book that makes me shiver, despite the frivolous tone and lightness of the telling. If viewed for even one second in a serious manner, this book would turn your blood to ice water. I have long thought comic genius arises from tragedy, think of Robin Williams or Richard Pryor, or think of Mark Twain. If you know his life, you know it must have very often been the case that he insisted on laughing to prevent crying. I’m not certain there ever existed a sharper wit or a more astute mind. <br /><br />You know I am going to be partial to anyone who would write this: <i>A home without a cat--and a well-fed, well-petted and properly revered cat--may be a perfect home, perhaps, but how can it prove title?</i><br /><br />One of the things I truly enjoyed about this particular novel were the entries into Pudd’nhead’s Calendar. A few examples:<br /><br /><i>Let us endeavor so to live that when we come to die even the undertaker will be sorry.<br /><br />The holy passion of friendship is of so sweet and steady and loyal and enduring a nature that it will last through a whole lifetime, if not asked to lend money.<br /><br />Consider well the proportions of things. It is better to be a young Jane-bug than an old bird of paradise.</i><br /><br />There are days when I particularly feel that last one!<br /><br />One of my favorite characters here was Roxy, the mother who does the switch-a-roo. She is a prime example of the person in charge might not be the person you think, and her quick mind saved the day more than once. Her weakness--that blasted kid.<br /><br />Glad to have finally read it. Wouldn't really want to have to say there was a Twain I had not experienced.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2317589007128179030" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2764572509" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2764572509" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/695116-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340192527p2/695116.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2764572509?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/695116-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781490611433" href="/book/show/37944574-pudd-nhead-wilson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=3classics">3classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=1audio">1audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=2fiction">2fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2764572509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3300860135686424549">I read this as a teen, so probably 45 years ago. I thought I remembered fairly well. Nope! Oh, I remembered the main points, but it was almost like a new book & really worth reading. This is Twain's answer to nature versus nurture while satirizing race, religion, 'honor', & small town life. While the destination is wonderful, it's the trip that is best.<br/><br/><b>The story is so well known enough that there will be spoilers in this review. </b><br/><br/>Tom & Chambers are switched at birth by their nurse who had her bab</span>
  <span id="freeText3300860135686424549" style="display:none">I read this as a teen, so probably 45 years ago. I thought I remembered fairly well. Nope! Oh, I remembered the main points, but it was almost like a new book &amp; really worth reading. This is Twain's answer to nature versus nurture while satirizing race, religion, 'honor', &amp; small town life. While the destination is wonderful, it's the trip that is best.<br /><br /><b>The story is so well known enough that there will be spoilers in this review. </b><br /><br />Tom &amp; Chambers are switched at birth by their nurse who had her baby the same day as her master's wife. She raises them both &amp; doesn't want to see her son endure a life of slavery, so she swaps them since no one else can tell them apart. Tom is 'white' while Chambers is 1/32 black, thus is 'black' by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/One-drop_rule" rel="nofollow noopener">the 'One Drop' rule</a> &amp; raised as a slave. (IIRC, this is about how much Indian blood Elizabeth Warren claimed.) The white child becomes a spoiled, cowardly, vicious despot while the black one becomes ignorant &amp; servile, but good of heart &amp; strong.<br /><br />The other main character is David "Pudd'nhead" Wilson. He makes a joke when he first comes to town &amp; it flies so high over the town folks' heads that they think he is serious, thus a pudd'nhead. It takes him 20 years (about 1830-50, the entire course of the novel) &amp; one of his foibles (a hobby of collecting fingerprints) to reclaim his reputation despite the constant evidence of his character &amp; intellect. He does so only through public spectacle. <br /><br />The use of fingerprints as the crux of the story is long foreshadowed for us, but I'm not sure how obvious it was in 1893 when this was published. It wasn't until about 1860 that fingerprints were documented in our current civilization (Chinese used them in 300BC.) In 1892, an Argentinian cop first used them to nail a murder according to <a href="http://www.fingerprinting.com/history-of-fingerprinting.php" rel="nofollow noopener">this quick history.</a> I recommend reading it. Interesting.<br /><br />One of the high points of the novel is each chapter beginning with an aphorism from Pudd'nhead Wilson's calendar, mimicking Poor Richard's. Many of Twain's best known come from this source.<br /><br />The characters are well voiced by Norman Dietz. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3300860135686424549" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_410744719" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/410744719" />
    <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart"><img alt="B. P. Rinehart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347740735p2/4635205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/410744719?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B. P. Rinehart" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart">B. P. Rinehart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=short-story-stuff">short-story-stuff</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer410744719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11942707575524371495">"<i>There are three infallible ways of pleasing an author, and<br/> the three form a rising scale of compliment: 1—to tell him<br/> you have read one of his books; 2—to tell him you have read<br/> all of his books; 3—to ask him to let you read the<br/> manuscript of his forthcoming book. No. 1 admits you to his<br/> respect; No. 2 admits you to his admiration; No. 3 carries<br/> you clear into his heart.</i>" —Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar <br/><br/>"<i>If you pick up a starving dog and make him prosperous, he will not bite you. This is the prin</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11942707575524371495" style="display:none">"<i>There are three infallible ways of pleasing an author, and<br /> the three form a rising scale of compliment: 1—to tell him<br /> you have read one of his books; 2—to tell him you have read<br /> all of his books; 3—to ask him to let you read the<br /> manuscript of his forthcoming book. No. 1 admits you to his<br /> respect; No. 2 admits you to his admiration; No. 3 carries<br /> you clear into his heart.</i>" —Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar <br /><br />"<i>If you pick up a starving dog and make him prosperous, he will not bite you. This is the principal difference between a dog and a man.</i>"<br />-Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar <br /><br />Nothing like some good ol' Mark Twain quotes. <br /><br /><br />This story is very short for a Twain novel and also very somber. It has his classic wittiness in it, but it is as its original title suggest, a <u>tragedy</u>. Mark Twain at this point had become as leftist as he would be in his career. He was deeply anti-imperialist (which made him a harsh critic of Presidents McKinley and Teddy Roosevelt) and he was much more liberal on race than many in his day (despite serving in a Confederate militia for a few weeks, he would marry into an abolitionist family and would be outspoken in near-equal rights for African-Americans) and his experiences with Slavery in his youth (his father was a part-time slave trader) would haunt and guilt him for much of his life and present a sort of dual nature within him. <br /><br />This book is set in Missouri (Mark Twain's home state) and makes great use of standard Twain motifs like the Steamboat, the Mississippi River, and smart-ass dialogue; it is extremely different in its seriousness and heart-breaking look at slavery. The institution is the real villain here because it implicitly and explicitly touches and destroys the lives of almost everyone involved in this story. <br /><br />Also this book, I think, breaks barriers because the <u>title</u> character is a Scotsman and I can't think of any American title in which the big character is from the northern part of Great Britain. I also am very keen on how this novel reminds me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/128382.Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy" rel="nofollow noopener">Leo Tolstoy</a>'s short stories and I don't know any other Mark Twain work-not even <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/102564.The_Mysterious_Stranger" title="The Mysterious Stranger by Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">The Mysterious Stranger</a>-to use so many tropes from the realist genre and it makes me wonder if Twain was aware of it since he is much strongly influenced by the more popular Victorian style that dominated the Anglo-sphere and is what he primarily worked out of. <br /><br />In the end this novella, partly inspired by a Black woman who lived on as neighbor to one of his in-laws, is to me a more serious,somber look at slavery than "<i>Huck Finn</i>" and anyone who wanted a substitute to that book ought to take a look at this one. <br /><br />"<i>Few things are harder to put up with than the annoyance of a good example.</i>"<br />-Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar <br /><br />"<i>APRIL 1. This is the day upon which we are reminded of what we are on the other three hundred and sixty-four.</i>"<br />-Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar <br /><br />"<i>October. This is one of the particularly dangerous months to speculate in stocks in. The others are July, January, September, April, November, May, March, June, December, August and February.</i>"<br />-Pudd'nhead Wilson's Calendar <br /><br />P.S.: Each chapter begins with one or two sayings from Dave "Pudd'nhead" Wilson's Almanac/Calendar. They are all hilarious.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11942707575524371495" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_374344112" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/374344112" />
    <a title="Joe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9777942-joe"><img alt="Joe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422026319p2/9777942.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/374344112?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/9777942-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer374344112" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9490798559974123900"><u>Pudd'nhead Wilson</u> is a brisk concoction; mixing adventure, mystery, and social commentary. Mark Twain trains his wit upon the arbitrary nature of 19th-century slave laws and rightfully skewers that wicked institution. But even as Twain's righteous humor often finds the mark, he nearly as often proves his naivete, as when he appears to condone another brutish custom; 'honorable' duels to the death.<br/><br/>Elsewhere, Twain cleverly explains the forensic power of fingerprints (before they were commonly use</span>
  <span id="freeText9490798559974123900" style="display:none"><u>Pudd'nhead Wilson</u> is a brisk concoction; mixing adventure, mystery, and social commentary. Mark Twain trains his wit upon the arbitrary nature of 19th-century slave laws and rightfully skewers that wicked institution. But even as Twain's righteous humor often finds the mark, he nearly as often proves his naivete, as when he appears to condone another brutish custom; 'honorable' duels to the death.<br /><br />Elsewhere, Twain cleverly explains the forensic power of fingerprints (before they were commonly used in criminal investigation) but also ascribes scientific power to the flimflammery of palmistry. Even the title feels odd; the nominal Mr. Wilson isn't the main character. He's an educated man who solves the mystery during the denouement, but he doesn't get that much page-time and doesn't drive the action. This novel would be more appropriately titled; Whiny Villain Tom Driskoll.<br /><br />But this is still an entertaining read; there's no shortage of drama or action (though the numerous turns of fate often feel arbitrary.) And the courtroom climax makes for a smashing conclusion.<br /><br /><i>Edited 9/30/2020</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="9490798559974123900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1596563575" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1596563575" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1596563575?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=19th-c-amer">19th-c-amer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=detective-mystery">detective-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1596563575" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15805232424216267114"><br/>If you consider a mans “best” books to be the ones with the most consistent tone and the fewest flaws, then <i>Tom Sawyer</i> and <i>The Prince and the Pauper</i> are Mark Twain’s best works of fiction. If, however, “best” means the most interesting, the most resonant, even if the flaws are considerable and the results problematic, then that honor belongs to <i>A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur’s Court</i>,” <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> are—I would argue—<i>The Tragedy of Puddin’head Wilson</i> too.<br/><br/>The flaws and the problems of Twai</span>
  <span id="freeText15805232424216267114" style="display:none"><br />If you consider a mans “best” books to be the ones with the most consistent tone and the fewest flaws, then <i>Tom Sawyer</i> and <i>The Prince and the Pauper</i> are Mark Twain’s best works of fiction. If, however, “best” means the most interesting, the most resonant, even if the flaws are considerable and the results problematic, then that honor belongs to <i>A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur’s Court</i>,” <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> are—I would argue—<i>The Tragedy of Puddin’head Wilson</i> too.<br /><br />The flaws and the problems of Twain’s fiction stem from the fact that the limited but parochial projects of Twain the humorist are often undermined and thwarted by the comprehensive soul of Twain the writer of fiction. In <i>Connecticut Yankee</i>, for example, as much as Twain admired Yankee know-how and despised the “jejune romanticism” of Sir Walter Scott, there was still a part of him that grudgingly admired Southern chivalry and was appalled by how Yankee know-how literally blew that chivalry apart on the great battlefields of the Civil War. For this reason, an essentially humorous book about a cunning modern inventor who outfoxes King Arthur’s finest ends with a bitter picture of modern warfare which considerably alters its tone. And the end of <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> exhibits similar problems in the comic—but essentially unfunny—return of Tom Sawyer to the narrative.<br /><br /><i>Puddin’head Wilson</i>—a smaller but equally resonant work—is comparable problematic. It began as a novel with the title <i>Those Extraordinary Twins</i>, featuring a pair of conjoined twins based on a well known Italian pair, Giovanni and Giacomo Tocci. Twain wished to contrast their relatively happy life with the dark story of two little Missouri boys growing up in the small town of Dawson’s Landing in the years before the Civil War. The two boys look much alike, but Tom is to be the master of the house, and Chambers is to be his slave. The story of how they are made to switch places, together with tale of Puddin’head Wilson, a lawyer who eventually resolves the mystery—if not the resulting tragedy—through the newly emerging science of fingerprinting, is a fascinating one. Unfortunately, it completely overwhelmed the story of the Italian twins. Twain decided, left them—unconjoined- to wander with little purpose through the narrative, a baffling vestige of his original comic conception.<br /><br />Still, it is a powerful narrative, particularly in its account of how the institution of slavery molds the characters of both the false master and the false slave. Twain’s touch is not always sure—there are even moments when Twain appears to be saying that even a drop “black blood’ may be enough to taint the human character—but at its basis this is a profound tale of the fatal effect of nurture versus nature, and how two boys switched at birth can be changed irrevocably, particularly when is slave and one is free.<br /><br />The novel isn’t perfect, but it is also a rattling good mystery, with a lot of good stuff about fingerprints, an exciting courtroom scene, and a wickedly ironic conclusion to the fate of the faux master. It’s got its problem, sure, but it is well worth a read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15805232424216267114" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_423334505" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/423334505" />
    <a title="P.V. LeForge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7026918-p-v-leforge"><img alt="P.V. LeForge" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442497459p2/7026918.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/423334505?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="P.V. LeForge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="P.V. LeForge" href="/user/show/7026918-p-v-leforge">P.V. LeForge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer423334505" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7945317114933720756"> Although I enjoyed reading <i>Pudd’nhead Wilson</i>, I enjoyed it more because Twain is generally enjoyable rather than because of anything remarkable about the book. In fact, I found the book to be more than a bit ragged. Hindsight is always easy, I know, but the knowledge of how the book came to be written and published points out the book’s flaws in a way that is hard to ignore.<br/><br/>	The book was to have been called “Those Extraordinary Twins,” and was to have been a farcical love story between a lightw</span>
  <span id="freeText7945317114933720756" style="display:none"> Although I enjoyed reading <i>Pudd’nhead Wilson</i>, I enjoyed it more because Twain is generally enjoyable rather than because of anything remarkable about the book. In fact, I found the book to be more than a bit ragged. Hindsight is always easy, I know, but the knowledge of how the book came to be written and published points out the book’s flaws in a way that is hard to ignore.<br /><br />	The book was to have been called “Those Extraordinary Twins,” and was to have been a farcical love story between a lightweight heroine named Rowena and one half of a pair of Siamese twins. An interesting idea and one that would not only have leant itself to many humorous opportunities but one that was staple Twain fare at the time. Twain was, after all, known as a humorist; he even called himself a “jackleg” novelist rather than a literary or serious one.<br /><br />	In 1893, Twain had already written all of his best works of long fiction. Although a famous man by that time, he had made several bad investments and, refusing to take advantage of bankruptcy laws, assumed a $100,000.00 debt. Although a world tour helped, the expenses of this tour were in themselves hefty, so it was necessary to sell something quickly. “Those Extraordinary Twins,” which he had begun several years earlier, was the thing closest at hand. Unfortunately, over the years, other characters had crept into his story and had literally taken it over. At the end of the new version, his original characters had become insignificant. He ended up taking out the entire original story of the twins, cut them apart to make them two whole men, and came away with the novel Pudd’nhead Wilson. Unfortunately, in his haste to publish and his creative time consumed with financial matters, Twain didn’t do as good a job of revising the manuscript and smoothing out the story as he could have. Several references to Luigi and Angelo as “freaks” were left in, both subtle and overt. Also left in is a scene where Luigi kicks Tom Driscoll over a bank of floodlights. Not only is the reason for Luigi’s anger unfathomable, but the idea of such a kick is improbable.<br /><br />	Does these omissions half-revisons ruin the book? Well, partly. The relationship between Tom Driscoll and Rowena is only hinted at, as if Twain did not want to take the responsibility of actually bringing that pair together, knowing what was to happen to Tom later. It is an unfortunate omission because it would have been interesting to see how he would have handled it. <br /><br />Four characters stand out in Pudd’nhead Wilson. Tom Driscoll is hateful almost from the moment of his birth and remains so. Wilson, called Puddn’head, is seems genuine and stable, although the reputation he has with the town is not as believable as it might be. He is obviously a smart man and well spoken. Judge Driscoll, the third important character, changes almost in mid-step. His feeling of warmth and friendship for the twins turns to disgust on only the word of his nephew, who has never shown the slightest tendency toward telling the truth or doing the right thing. Roxana, probably the strongest character in the book, changes as well. She is shown as a likeable young woman early in the book, yet becomes hard and deceitful, supporting Tom’s burglary activities. Of course, her baby was switched at birth with another. Did this harden her? Perhaps, but I would have liked to see it come out more gradually.<br /><br />	It is also disturbing that Twain seems to leave unchallenged Roxy’s assertion that having even a drop of black blood makes a person bad. Even if she had not said it, the example of Tom and Chambers would have made the same statement. And, of course, Roxy had her own streak of evil. Twain never mentioned what Roxy thought when she heard that it was her son that killed the judge. Would she have supported him in that, too, even though she wanted the twins executed for the deed when she thought that they were guilty? Another omission. <br /><br />	All in all, this book should be read by Twain aficionados only, and only as an example of what might have been. <br />	<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7945317114933720756" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3093796181" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3093796181" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/491443-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369756539p2/491443.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3093796181?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/491443-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22960651-pudd-nhead-wilson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/491443-john?shelf=southern-fiction">southern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/491443-john?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/491443-john?shelf=literary-classic">literary-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/491443-john?shelf=racism">racism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3093796181" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2348920647246969082">Pudd'nhead Wilson received his nickname because many townspeople considered him a fool. Although Pudd'nhead is not the protagonist, he plays an integral part to the story. I guess a subtitle could be, "How He Got His Name Back." <br/><br/>Mark Twain's tale involves a slave, 1/16th black, impregnated by the slave owner, Judge Driscoll. She gives birth to twins, now 1/32nd black, one to become the heir to the estate while the other to remain a slave. The mother switches the two at birth; therefore, the fate</span>
  <span id="freeText2348920647246969082" style="display:none">Pudd'nhead Wilson received his nickname because many townspeople considered him a fool. Although Pudd'nhead is not the protagonist, he plays an integral part to the story. I guess a subtitle could be, "How He Got His Name Back." <br /><br />Mark Twain's tale involves a slave, 1/16th black, impregnated by the slave owner, Judge Driscoll. She gives birth to twins, now 1/32nd black, one to become the heir to the estate while the other to remain a slave. The mother switches the two at birth; therefore, the fate of the two are reversed. As the heir, Tom Driscoll grows to manhood, he engages in profligate living resulting in significant gambling debts. His outlook on life is worsened when his mother appears and reveals that Tom is actually black. Despondent, he disguising himself as a woman breaking into wealthy homes to address his gambling debts. However, matters only become worse.<br /><br />Much of this book satirizes racism since the mother and twins essentially appear white. Another theme involves nature versus nurture or "clothes make the man." <br /><br />One of the reasons that Pudd'nhead receives his nickname is that he has a hobby of collecting fingerprints, which plays an important part in the climax of the book. Since this book was published in 1893, it is worthy to note that fingerprinting as a forensic tool had only been introduced a year earlier by Inspector Eduardo Alvarez in Argentina and Mark Twain incorporated it into the plot of this book.<br /><br />A lesser known book of Mark Twain I was glad I read. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2348920647246969082" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3148663239" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3148663239" />
    <a title="Camie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7399266-camie"><img alt="Camie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571025937p2/7399266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<div id="review_470316509" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/470316509" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/470316509?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who want something different</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to David by: </span>

  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=historical">historical</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer470316509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8954466289061338719"><b>The Show Trial</b><br/>21 January 2013<br/><br/>	I had never heard of this story until I purchased a Samuel Clements (aka Mark Twain) book that contained it with two of the stories of his (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2956.The_Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn__Tom_Sawyer___Huckleberry_Finn___2_' title='The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn (Tom Sawyer & Huckleberry Finn, #2) by Mark Twain' rel='nofollow noopener'>Huckleberry Finn</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24583.The_Adventures_of_Tom_Sawyer__Tom_Sawyer___Huckleberry_Finn___1_' title='The Adventures of Tom Sawyer (Tom Sawyer & Huckleberry Finn, #1) by Mark Twain' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tom Sawyer</a>) that I wanted to read (and it also contained the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/62446.The_Prince_and_the_Pauper' title='The Prince and the Pauper by Mark Twain' rel='nofollow noopener'>Prince and the Pauper</a>). In a way this story is very similar, but very different, to Prince and the Pauper. The similarities involve two boys that take each other's place, but that is pretty much where the similarities end. This story is set in the </span>
  <span id="freeText8954466289061338719" style="display:none"><b>The Show Trial</b><br />21 January 2013<br /><br />	I had never heard of this story until I purchased a Samuel Clements (aka Mark Twain) book that contained it with two of the stories of his (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2956.The_Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn__Tom_Sawyer___Huckleberry_Finn___2_" title="The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn (Tom Sawyer &amp; Huckleberry Finn, #2) by Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Huckleberry Finn</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24583.The_Adventures_of_Tom_Sawyer__Tom_Sawyer___Huckleberry_Finn___1_" title="The Adventures of Tom Sawyer (Tom Sawyer &amp; Huckleberry Finn, #1) by Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Tom Sawyer</a>) that I wanted to read (and it also contained the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/62446.The_Prince_and_the_Pauper" title="The Prince and the Pauper by Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Prince and the Pauper</a>). In a way this story is very similar, but very different, to Prince and the Pauper. The similarities involve two boys that take each other's place, but that is pretty much where the similarities end. This story is set in the United States and the two boys are switched at birth, not by accident, but deliberately. Further, the story only focuses on one of the boys, since the switch involved a pure white baby and a baby that was 1/32 part Negro, but because of that really minor part that was Negro, he was still considered a Negro.<br /><br />	The character whom the title is named after, David Wilson, really takes a back seat for most of the story, and only comes to the fore in the last few chapters when he is finally given the chance to prove his worth. Basically Pudd'nhead is a lawyer that moves out to a small town south of St Louis and on his first day makes a stupid comment and is then cursed with the name Pudd'nhead, which basically means stupid. Pudd'nhead is more eccentric than stupid, and one of the things that makes him eccentric, his collection of fingerprints, is what ends up turning him around and making him a hero.<br /><br />	Some have said that this story is a courtroom drama, but most of the comments that I have read about it have suggested that it is not. While there is a courtroom scene, it only makes up a small part of the story, though much of the story builds up to this scene. In a courtroom drama the murder is usually commented near the beginning, or even before the story begins. However the murder in this story does not occur until near the end, and while it is clear why the murder was committed (money and unpaid gambling debts) it is more like an anti-climax.<br /><br />	The thing that impressed me the most about this story though was that forensic fingerprinting played a very major role in an era before fingerprinting was actually accepted as evidence in court. However remember, this is a small town in rural America, and as such courtroom scenes become more like some sort of show (as is indicated in this book) than some really serious matter as one would expect in the city. Remember, everybody knows everybody else, including the judge, and it is basically the person that performs the best show that wins the trial.<br /><br />	However, that is still very much the case today. Trials are less to do with actually finding the truth and more to do with who tells the more believable story, and who the judge prefers to believe. In my time in personal injury litigation there are always stories about soft judges and hard judges. This is basically determined by who likes plaintiffs and who hates plaintiffs, and even then, their word is never final. When somebody else ends up paying the court fees, the ability of plaintiffs to actually prosecute their case increases.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8954466289061338719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_396383622" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396383622" />
    <a title="Dusty" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/655483-dusty"><img alt="Dusty" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605501426p2/655483.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/396383622?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dusty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dusty" href="/user/show/655483-dusty">Dusty</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/655483-dusty?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer396383622" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3313859788083829012">Mark Twain wrote this novel when he was pretty old, pretty crabby, and living in Europe to avoid creditors and the other people who made him feel old and crabby. Really, it's a simple story: A light-skinned slave woman swaps her baby with her master's baby, hoping to ensure the former a happier life without the risk of being "sold down the river," and the rest of the book builds suspense for the "big moment" when true identities are revealed. <br/><br/>I've read a few reviews that allege that <i>Pudd'nhead W</i></span>
  <span id="freeText3313859788083829012" style="display:none">Mark Twain wrote this novel when he was pretty old, pretty crabby, and living in Europe to avoid creditors and the other people who made him feel old and crabby. Really, it's a simple story: A light-skinned slave woman swaps her baby with her master's baby, hoping to ensure the former a happier life without the risk of being "sold down the river," and the rest of the book builds suspense for the "big moment" when true identities are revealed. <br /><br />I've read a few reviews that allege that <i>Pudd'nhead Wilson</i> is a book against slavery, but this isn't quite accurate, as the Civil War had made slavery effectively illegal a quarter century before it was published. Instead, Twain writes a book to counter "scientific" claims made in the late nineteenth century in support of (white) racial superiority. Slavery is over, he argues, but people who believe nature (one's heritage) has a larger impact than nurture (the social conditions into which one is born) are essentially slave-holders in their modern reincarnations. On one hand, I'd like to fault Twain for writing a book ostensibly about being black in the United States without including a single character whose skin is noticeably dark. But on the other hand, it's a treat how he points out real and artificial differences between white skins. There is, after all, less difference in color between "white" master and "black" slave than there is between the Italian twins who move into town, who are able to be distinguished only because one is slightly "darker" than the other.<br /><br />Twain stages his conclusion in a courtroom, where poor Pudd'nhead Wilson finally has the chance to practice the law he studied oh-so-many years ago. For those of us who're accustomed to (and extremely tired of) the courtroom formula that plays out every week in <i>Law and Order</i> and a dozen other TV series, I think this is a bit of a let-down after what is otherwise an imaginative and caustic romp. However, I can't deny that that "big moment," when it finally happens, is written masterfully. Four stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3313859788083829012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1379637116" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1379637116" />
    <a title="Tom Mathews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22063432-tom-mathews"><img alt="Tom Mathews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601835093p2/22063432.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1379637116?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Mathews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Mathews" href="/user/show/22063432-tom-mathews">Tom Mathews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451508409" href="/book/show/3032445-pudd-nhead-wilson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22063432-tom-mathews?shelf=read-before-2010">read-before-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22063432-tom-mathews?shelf=oldies-but-goodies">oldies-but-goodies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22063432-tom-mathews?shelf=group-reads">group-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22063432-tom-mathews?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1379637116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5186607863468949782">This entertaining American version of H.M.S Pinafore is a good book that suffers greatly from having to exist in the shadow of its more adventurous siblings, Tom Sawyer and Huckleberry Finn. Like its English cousin, it uses the old switched-in-the-cradle plot device to explore the subject of class divides, in this case racial. Sadly it also reveals a good deal about what Mark Twain himself thought about the subject which, despite his commendably progressive attitudes, still reflects an acceptanc</span>
  <span id="freeText5186607863468949782" style="display:none">This entertaining American version of H.M.S Pinafore is a good book that suffers greatly from having to exist in the shadow of its more adventurous siblings, Tom Sawyer and Huckleberry Finn. Like its English cousin, it uses the old switched-in-the-cradle plot device to explore the subject of class divides, in this case racial. Sadly it also reveals a good deal about what Mark Twain himself thought about the subject which, despite his commendably progressive attitudes, still reflects an acceptance in physiological differences between races that have since been discredited.<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <br />Although the changeling angle makes up most of the story Twain also brings up the subject of fingerprinting. Although the science of fingerprinting has been around for centuries, it was not used as a criminal forensic tool until 1892, when it was used to solve a murder in Argentina. Twain may well have read of this and incorporated into Pudd’nHead Wilson, which was first published one year later, in 1893.<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />Bottom line: This is not a perfect story so I can’t give it five stars but it is entertaining and it was also written by Mark Twain, who is one of the few writers whose shopping lists I would happily read. I definitely recommend this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5186607863468949782" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3307968623" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3307968623" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1385615-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536121261p2/1385615.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3307968623?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/1385615-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3307968623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16236880375702244845">Mark Twain's fiction has always left me feeling conflicted, and this has not improved with age. His incredible insights into human behavior, and his ability to dissect the (dys)functions of small town society, still pack a punch. He was a talented humorist and pithy philosopher. His portrayal of rural Black folk, on the other hand, has become more noxious with time. It's simply not possible to pass off these usually undignified, vulgar, shifty portrayals as "character studies". It's pretty clear</span>
  <span id="freeText16236880375702244845" style="display:none">Mark Twain's fiction has always left me feeling conflicted, and this has not improved with age. His incredible insights into human behavior, and his ability to dissect the (dys)functions of small town society, still pack a punch. He was a talented humorist and pithy philosopher. His portrayal of rural Black folk, on the other hand, has become more noxious with time. It's simply not possible to pass off these usually undignified, vulgar, shifty portrayals as "character studies". It's pretty clear that he thought "coloreds" - slave or free - were worthy of better treatment than they were receiving at the time, sure, but also not remotely the equals of White Americans or Europeans.<br /><br />This is a great yarn well told. The plot is interesting and the pacing excellent. The entries from Pudd'nhead Wilson's almanac are marvelous. What a shame it's shot through with racist sentiments.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16236880375702244845" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2066797127" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23841736-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532221628p2/23841736.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/23841736-sarah">Sarah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
